<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Space Shuttle Columbia disaster</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_disaster&amp;redirect=no" title="Columbia disaster">Columbia disaster</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em">
<caption class="summary">Space Shuttle Columbia disaster</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;border-bottom:#aaa solid 1px">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg"><img alt="STS-107 Flight Insignia.svg" data-file-height="1757" data-file-width="1303" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg/220px-STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg/330px-STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg/440px-STS-107_Flight_Insignia.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div>

<td style="text-align: left;">February 1, 2003<span class="noprint">; 14 years ago</span><span style="display:none"> (<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2003-02-01</span>)</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Time</th>
<td style="text-align: left;">08:59 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_American_Eastern_Time_Zone" title="North American Eastern Time Zone">EST</a> (13:59 <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a>)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Location</th>

</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Cause</th>
<td style="text-align: left;">Wing damage from debris</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Outcome</th>
<td class="description" style="text-align: left;">Space Shuttle fleet was grounded for more than two years while safety measures were added, including procedures to deal with catastrophic cabin depressurization, better crew restraints, and an automated parachute system.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:lavender;">Casualties</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left;">
<div class="plainlist">
<ul>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Rick_Husband" title="Rick Husband">Rick D. Husband</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/William_C._McCool" title="William C. McCool">William C. McCool</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Michael_P._Anderson" title="Michael P. Anderson">Michael P. Anderson</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Kalpana_Chawla" title="Kalpana Chawla">Kalpana Chawla</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/David_M._Brown" title="David M. Brown">David M. Brown</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Laurel_Clark" title="Laurel Clark">Laurel Clark</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Ilan_Ramon" title="Ilan Ramon">Ilan Ramon</a></span></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Inquiries</th>
<td style="text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board" title="Columbia Accident Investigation Board">Columbia Accident Investigation Board</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On February 1, 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia">Space Shuttle <i>Columbia</i></a> disintegrated upon <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">reentering</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, killing all seven crew members.</p>
<p>The disaster was the second fatal accident in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a> after <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Space Shuttle <i>Challenger</i></a>, which <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">broke apart</a> and killed the seven-member crew 73 seconds after liftoff in 1986.</p>
<p>During the launch of <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">STS-107</a>, <i>Columbia'</i>s 28th mission, a piece of foam insulation broke off from the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">Space Shuttle external tank</a> and struck the left wing of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_orbiter" title="Space Shuttle orbiter">orbiter</a>. A few previous shuttle launches had seen damage ranging from minor to major from foam shedding,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foamstrikes_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-foamstrikes-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> but some engineers suspected that the damage to <i>Columbia</i> was more serious. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> managers limited the investigation, reasoning that the crew could not have fixed the problem if it had been confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>When <i>Columbia</i> re-entered the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere of Earth</a>, the damage allowed hot atmospheric gases to penetrate and destroy the internal wing structure, which caused the spacecraft to become unstable and break apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USATODAY_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-USATODAY-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the disaster, Space Shuttle flight operations were suspended for more than two years, as they had been after the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"><i>Challenger</i> disaster</a>. Construction of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) was put on hold; the station relied entirely on the Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roscosmos_State_Corporation" title="Roscosmos State Corporation">Roscosmos State Corporation</a> for resupply for 29 months until Shuttle flights resumed with <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a> and 41 months for crew rotation until <a href="/wiki/STS-121" title="STS-121">STS-121</a>.</p>
<p>Several technical and organizational changes were made, including adding a thorough on-orbit inspection to determine how well the shuttle's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle_thermal_protection_system" title="Space shuttle thermal protection system">thermal protection system</a> had endured the ascent, and keeping a designated rescue mission ready in case irreparable damage was found. Except for <a href="/wiki/STS-125" title="STS-125">one final mission</a> to repair the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, subsequent shuttle missions were flown only to the ISS so that the crew could use it as a haven in case damage to the orbiter prevented safe reentry.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew">Crew</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Crew">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crew_of_STS-107,_official_photo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3277" data-file-width="4096" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Crew_of_STS-107%2C_official_photo.jpg/220px-Crew_of_STS-107%2C_official_photo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Crew_of_STS-107%2C_official_photo.jpg/330px-Crew_of_STS-107%2C_official_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Crew_of_STS-107%2C_official_photo.jpg/440px-Crew_of_STS-107%2C_official_photo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crew_of_STS-107,_official_photo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The crew of <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">STS-107</a> in October 2001. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/David_M._Brown" title="David M. Brown">Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rick_Husband" title="Rick Husband">Husband</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurel_Clark" title="Laurel Clark">Clark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalpana_Chawla" title="Kalpana Chawla">Chawla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_P._Anderson" title="Michael P. Anderson">Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_C._McCool" title="William C. McCool">McCool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilan_Ramon" title="Ilan Ramon">Ramon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Commander: <a href="/wiki/Rick_Husband" title="Rick Husband">Rick D. Husband</a>, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">colonel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_engineering" title="Mechanical engineering">mechanical engineer</a>, who piloted a previous shuttle during the first docking with the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (<a href="/wiki/STS-96" title="STS-96">STS-96</a>).</li>
<li>Pilot: <a href="/wiki/William_C._McCool" title="William C. McCool">William C. McCool</a>, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Commander_(United_States)" title="Commander (United States)">commander</a>.</li>
<li>Payload Commander: <a href="/wiki/Michael_P._Anderson" title="Michael P. Anderson">Michael P. Anderson</a>, a U.S. Air Force <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_colonel_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant colonel (United States)">lieutenant colonel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mission_specialist" title="Mission specialist">mission specialist</a> who was in charge of the science mission.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Payload_Specialist" title="Payload Specialist">Payload Specialist</a>: <a href="/wiki/Ilan_Ramon" title="Ilan Ramon">Ilan Ramon</a>, a colonel in the <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Air_Force" title="Israeli Air Force">Israeli Air Force</a> and the first Israeli <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronaut</a>.</li>
<li>Mission Specialist: <a href="/wiki/Kalpana_Chawla" title="Kalpana Chawla">Kalpana Chawla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">aerospace engineer</a> who was on her second space mission.</li>
<li>Mission Specialist: <a href="/wiki/David_M._Brown" title="David M. Brown">David M. Brown</a>, a U.S. Navy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(U.S._Navy)" title="Captain (U.S. Navy)">captain</a> trained as an aviator and <a href="/wiki/Flight_surgeon" title="Flight surgeon">flight surgeon</a>. Brown worked on scientific experiments.</li>
<li>Mission Specialist: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurel_Blair_Salton_Clark" title="Laurel Blair Salton Clark">Laurel Blair Salton Clark</a>, a U.S. Navy captain and flight surgeon. Clark worked on biological experiments.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debris_strike_during_launch">Debris strike during launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Debris strike during launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2024" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg/170px-Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg/255px-Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg/340px-Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Close-up_STS-107_Launch_-_GPN-2003-00080.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Columbia</i> lifting off on its final mission. The light-colored triangle visible at the base of the strut near the nose of the orbiter is the Left Bipod Foam Ramp. <a class="external text" href="http://science.ksc.nasa.gov/shuttle/missions/sts-107/movies/sts-107-launch.rm" rel="nofollow">Video</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG/220px-Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG/330px-Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG/440px-Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_external_tank_foam_block.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Space Shuttle external tank foam block</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="984" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg/220px-Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg/330px-Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg/440px-Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Left_bipod_foam_ramp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Close-up of the Left Bipod Foam Ramp that broke off and damaged the Shuttle wing</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The shuttle's <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">main fuel tank</a> is covered in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">thermal insulation</a> foam intended to prevent ice from forming when the tank is full of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">oxygen</a>. Such ice could damage the shuttle if shed during lift-off.</p>
<p>Mission <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">STS-107</a> was the 113th Space Shuttle launch. Planned to begin on January 11, 2001, the mission was delayed 18 times<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mission_objectives_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-mission_objectives-5">[5]</a></sup> and eventually launched on January 16, 2003, following <a href="/wiki/STS-113" title="STS-113">STS-113</a>. (The Columbia Accident Investigation Board determined that this delay had nothing to do with the catastrophic failure.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mission_objectives_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-mission_objectives-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 82 seconds after launch from <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>'s LC-39-A, a suitcase-sized piece of foam broke off from the External Tank (ET), striking <i>Columbia</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s left wing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reinforced_carbon-carbon" title="Reinforced carbon-carbon">reinforced carbon-carbon</a> (RCC) panels. As demonstrated by ground experiments conducted by the Columbia Accident Investigation Board, this likely created a 6-to-10-inch (15 to 25 cm) diameter hole, allowing hot gases to enter the wing when <i>Columbia</i> later re-entered the atmosphere. At the time of the foam strike, the orbiter was at an altitude of about 66,000 feet (20 km; 12.5 mi), traveling at Mach 2.46 (1,626 miles per hour or 727 meters per second).</p>
<p>The Left Bipod Foam Ramp is an approximately three-foot (one-meter) long aerodynamic component made entirely of foam. The foam, not normally considered to be a structural material, is required to bear some aerodynamic loads. Because of these special requirements, the casting-in-place and curing of the ramps may be performed only by a senior technician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CoF_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-CoF-6">[6]</a></sup> The bipod ramp (having left and right sides) was originally designed to reduce aerodynamic stresses around the bipod attachment points at the external tank, but it was proven unnecessary in the wake of the accident and was removed from the external tank design for tanks flown after STS-107 (another foam ramp along the liquid oxygen line was also later removed from the tank design to eliminate it as a foam debris source, after analysis and tests proved this change safe).</p>
<p>Bipod Ramp insulation had been observed falling off, in whole or in part, on four previous flights: <a href="/wiki/STS-7" title="STS-7">STS-7</a> (1983), <a href="/wiki/STS-32" title="STS-32">STS-32</a> (1990), <a href="/wiki/STS-50" title="STS-50">STS-50</a> (1992) and most recently <a href="/wiki/STS-112" title="STS-112">STS-112</a> (just two launches before STS-107). All affected shuttle missions completed successfully. NASA management came to refer to this phenomenon as "foam shedding". As with the <a href="/wiki/O-ring" title="O-ring">O-ring</a> erosion problems that ultimately doomed the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Space Shuttle <i>Challenger</i></a>, NASA management became accustomed to these phenomena when no serious consequences resulted from these earlier episodes. This phenomenon was termed "normalization of deviance" by sociologist <a href="/wiki/Diane_Vaughan" title="Diane Vaughan">Diane Vaughan</a> in her book on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_launch_decision" title="Space Shuttle Challenger launch decision"><i>Challenger</i> launch decision process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>As it happened, STS-112 had been the first flight with the "ET Cam", a video feed mounted on the ET for the purpose of giving greater insight to the foam shedding problem. During that launch a chunk of foam broke away from the ET bipod ramp and hit the SRB-ET Attach Ring near the bottom of the left <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">solid rocket booster (SRB)</a> causing a dent four inches wide and three inches deep in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STS-112_SRB_Foam_Damage_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-STS-112_SRB_Foam_Damage-8">[8]</a></sup> After STS-112, NASA leaders analyzed the situation and decided to press ahead under the justification that "[t]he ET is safe to fly with no new concerns (and no added risk)" of further foam strikes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jerry_Smelser_flight_rationale_ppt_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jerry_Smelser_flight_rationale_ppt-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Video taken during lift-off of STS-107 was routinely reviewed two hours later and revealed nothing unusual. The following day, higher-resolution film that had been processed overnight revealed the foam debris striking the left wing, potentially damaging the thermal protection on the Space Shuttle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> At the time, the exact location where the foam struck the wing could not be determined due to the low resolution of the tracking camera footage.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, NASA's judgment about the risks was revisited. <a href="/wiki/Linda_Ham" title="Linda Ham">Linda Ham</a>, chair of the Mission Management Team (MMT), said, "Rationale was lousy then and still is." Ham and Shuttle Program Manager <a href="/wiki/Ron_Dittemore" title="Ron Dittemore">Ron Dittemore</a> had both been present at the October 31, 2002, meeting where the decision to continue with launches was made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Post-disaster analysis revealed that two previous shuttle launches (<a href="/wiki/STS-52" title="STS-52">STS-52</a> and <a href="/wiki/STS-62" title="STS-62">-62</a>) also had bipod ramp foam loss that went undetected. In addition, Protuberance Air Load (PAL) ramp foam had also shed pieces, and there were also spot losses from large-area foams.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_risk_management">Flight risk management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Flight risk management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In a risk-management scenario similar to the <i>Challenger</i> disaster, NASA management failed to recognize the relevance of engineering concerns for safety and suggestions for imaging to inspect possible damage, and failed to respond to engineers' requests about the status of astronaut inspection of the left wing. Engineers made three separate requests for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> (DOD) imaging of the shuttle in orbit to more precisely determine damage. While the images were not guaranteed to show the damage, the capability existed for imaging of sufficient resolution to provide meaningful examination. NASA management did not honor the requests and in some cases intervened to stop the DOD from assisting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The CAIB recommended subsequent shuttle flights be imaged while in orbit using ground-based or space-based DOD assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Details of the DOD's unfulfilled participation with <i>Columbia</i> remain secret; retired NASA official <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Hale" title="Wayne Hale">Wayne Hale</a> stated in 2012 that "activity regarding other national assets and agencies remains classified and I cannot comment on that aspect of the Columbia tragedy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hale20120418_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-hale20120418-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the risk assessment process, senior NASA managers were influenced by their belief that nothing could be done even if damage were detected. This affected their stance on investigation urgency, thoroughness and possible contingency actions. They decided to conduct a parametric "what-if" scenario study more suited to determine risk probabilities of future events, instead of inspecting and assessing the actual damage. The investigation report in particular singled out NASA manager <a href="/wiki/Linda_Ham" title="Linda Ham">Linda Ham</a> for exhibiting this attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In 2013, Hale recalled that Director of Mission Operations Jon C. Harpold told him before <i>Columbia<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span></i>s destruction:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>You know, there is nothing we can do about damage to the TPS [Thermal Protection System]. If it has been damaged it's probably better not to know. I think the crew would rather not know. Don't you think it would be better for them to have a happy successful flight and die unexpectedly during entry than to stay on orbit, knowing that there was nothing to be done, until the air ran out?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hale20130113_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-hale20130113-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Much of the risk assessment hinged on damage predictions to the thermal protection system. These fall into two categories: damage to the silica tile on the wing lower surface, and damage to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reinforced_carbon-carbon" title="Reinforced carbon-carbon">reinforced carbon-carbon</a> (RCC) leading-edge panels. The TPS includes a third category of components, thermal insulating blankets, but damage predictions are not typically performed on them. Damage assessments on the thermal blankets can be performed after an anomaly has been observed, and this has been done at least once after the return to flight following Columbia's loss.</p>
<p>Before the flight NASA believed that the RCC was very durable. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_F._Bolden" title="Charles F. Bolden">Charles F. Bolden</a>, who worked on tile-damage scenarios and repair methods early in his astronaut career, said in 2004 that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bolden20040106_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-bolden20040106-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>never did we talk about [the RCC] because we all thought that it was impenetrable ... I spent fourteen years in the space program flying, thinking that I had this huge mass that was about five or six inches thick on the leading edge of the wing. And, to find after Columbia that it was fractions of an inch thick, and that it wasn't as strong as the Fiberglass on your <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevy_Corvette" title="Chevy Corvette">Corvette</a>, that was an eye-opener, and I think for all of us ... the best minds that I know of, in and outside of NASA, never envisioned that as a failure mode.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Damage-prediction software was used to evaluate possible tile and RCC damage. The tool for predicting tile damage was known as "Crater", described by several NASA representatives in press briefings as not actually a software program but rather a statistical spreadsheet of observed past flight events and effects. The "Crater" tool predicted severe penetration of multiple tiles by the impact if it struck the TPS tile area, but NASA engineers downplayed this. It had been shown that the model overstated damage from small projectiles, and engineers believed that the model would also overstate damage from larger Spray-On Foam Insulation (SOFI) impacts. The program used to predict RCC damage was based on small ice impacts the size of cigarette butts, not larger SOFI impacts, as the ice impacts were the only recognized threats to RCC panels up to that point. Under one of 15 predicted SOFI impact paths, the software predicted an ice impact would completely penetrate the RCC panel. Engineers downplayed this, too, believing that impacts of the less dense SOFI material would result in less damage than ice impacts. In an e-mail exchange, NASA managers questioned whether the density of the SOFI could be used as justification for reducing predicted damage. Despite engineering concerns about the energy imparted by the SOFI material, NASA managers ultimately accepted the rationale to reduce predicted damage of the RCC panels from possible complete penetration to slight damage to the panel's thin coating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ultimately the NASA Mission Management Team felt there was insufficient evidence to indicate that the strike was an unsafe situation, so they declared the debris strike a "turnaround" issue (not of highest importance) and denied the requests for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> images.</p>
<p>On January 23, flight director Steve Stich sent an e-mail to <i>Columbia</i>, informing commander Husband and pilot McCool of the foam strike while unequivocally dismissing any concerns about entry safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>During ascent at approximately 80 seconds, photo analysis shows that some debris from the area of the -Y ET Bipod Attach Point came loose and subsequently impacted the orbiter left wing, in the area of transition from Chine to Main Wing, creating a shower of smaller particles. The impact appears to be totally on the lower surface and no particles are seen to traverse over the upper surface of the wing. Experts have reviewed the high speed photography and there is no concern for RCC or tile damage. We have seen this same phenomenon on several other flights and there is absolutely no concern for entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Edward Tufte, an expert in information design and presentation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wamo_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-wamo-22">[22]</a></sup> remarked on poor modes of communication during the assessment made on the ground, before Columbia's reentry. NASA and Boeing favored Microsoft PowerPoint for conveying information. PowerPoint uses multi-level bullet points and orients towards single-page-of-information groupings. This is not ideal for complex scientific and engineering reports, and may have caused recipients to draw incorrect conclusions. In particular, the slide format may have emphasized optimistic options and glossed over the more accurate pessimistic viewpoints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-entry_timeline">Re-entry timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Re-entry timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Columbia</i> was scheduled to land at 9:16 a.m. EST.</p>
<ul>
<li><i>2:30 a.m. EST, February 1, 2003</i>: The Entry <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller" title="Flight controller">Flight Control Team</a> began duty in the Mission Control Center (known today as the <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center</a>).</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The Flight Control Team had not been working on any issues or problems related to the planned de-orbit and re-entry of <i>Columbia</i>. In particular, the team had indicated no concerns about the debris that hit the left wing during ascent, and treated the re-entry like any other. The team worked through the de-orbit preparation checklist and re-entry checklist procedures. Weather forecasters, with the help of pilots in the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Training_Aircraft" title="Shuttle Training Aircraft">Shuttle Training Aircraft</a>, evaluated landing-site weather conditions at the Kennedy Space Center.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:00</i>: Mission Control Center Entry Flight Director <a href="/wiki/LeRoy_E._Cain" title="LeRoy E. Cain">LeRoy Cain</a> polled the Mission Control room for a GO/NO-GO decision for the de-orbit burn.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>All weather observations and forecasts were within guidelines set by the flight rules, and all systems were normal.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:10</i>: The Capsule Communicator (CAPCOM) told the crew that they were GO for de-orbit burn.</li>
<li><i>8:15:30 (EI-1719)</i>: Husband and McCool executed the de-orbit burn using <i>Columbia</i>’s two Orbital Maneuvering System engines.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The Orbiter was upside down and tail-first over the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> at an altitude of 175 miles (282 km) and speed of 17,500 miles per hour (7.8 km/s) when the burn was executed. A 2-minute, 38-second de-orbit burn during the 255th orbit slowed the Orbiter to begin its re-entry into the atmosphere. The burn proceeded normally, putting the crew under about one-tenth gravity. Husband then turned <i>Columbia</i> right side up, facing forward with the nose pitched up.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:44:09 (EI+000)</i>: Entry Interface (EI), arbitrarily defined as the point at which the Orbiter entered the discernible atmosphere at 400,000 feet (120 km; 76 mi), occurred over the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>As <i>Columbia</i> descended, the heat of reentry caused wing leading-edge temperatures to rise steadily, reaching an estimated 2,500 °F (1,370 °C) during the next six minutes. (As former Space Shuttle Program Manager Wayne Hale said in a press briefing, about 90% of this heating is the result of compression of the atmospheric gas caused by the orbiter's supersonic flight, rather than the result of friction.)</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg/220px-STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg/330px-STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg/440px-STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-107_Columbia_entry_imaged_from_ground.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Columbia</i> at about 8:57. Debris is visible coming from the left wing (bottom). The image was taken at <a href="/wiki/Starfire_Optical_Range" title="Starfire Optical Range">Starfire Optical Range</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kirtland_Air_Force_Base" title="Kirtland Air Force Base">Kirtland Air Force Base</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i>8:48:39 (EI+270)</i>: A sensor on the left wing leading edge spar showed strains higher than those seen on previous <i>Columbia</i> re-entries.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>This was recorded only on the Modular Auxiliary Data System, which is similar in concept to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_data_recorder" title="Flight data recorder">flight data recorder</a>, and was not sent to ground controllers or shown to the crew.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:49:32 (EI+323)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> executed a planned roll to the right. Speed: <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a> 24.5.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><i>Columbia</i> began a banking turn to manage lift and therefore limit the Orbiter's rate of descent and heating.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:50:53 (EI+404)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> entered a 10-minute period of peak heating, during which the thermal stresses were at their maximum. Speed: Mach 24.1; altitude: 243,000 feet (74 km; 46.0 mi).</li>
<li><i>8:52:00 (EI+471)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> was about 300 miles (480 km) west of the <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> coastline.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The wing leading-edge temperatures usually reached 2,650 °F (1,450 °C) at this point.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:53:26 (EI+557)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> crossed the California coast west of <a href="/wiki/Sacramento,_California" title="Sacramento, California">Sacramento</a>. Speed: Mach 23; altitude: 231,600 feet (70.6 km; 43.86 mi).</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="621" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg/220px-Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg/330px-Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg/440px-Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbia_debris_detected_by_radar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Columbia</i> debris (in red, orange, and yellow) detected by <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> radar over Texas and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>The Orbiter's wing leading edge typically reached more than 2,800 °F (1,540 °C) at this point.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:53:46 (EI+577)</i>: Various people on the ground saw signs of debris being shed. Speed: Mach 22.8; altitude: 230,200 feet (70.2 km; 43.60 mi).</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The superheated air surrounding the Orbiter suddenly brightened, causing a streak in the Orbiter's luminescent trail that was quite noticeable in the pre-dawn skies over the West Coast. Observers witnessed four similar events during the following 23 seconds. Dialogue on some of the amateur footage indicates the observers were aware of the abnormality of what they were filming.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:54:24 (EI+615)</i>: The Maintenance, Mechanical, and Crew Systems (MMACS) officer told the Flight Director that four hydraulic sensors in the left wing were indicating "off-scale low". In Mission Control, re-entry had been proceeding normally up to this point.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>"Off-scale low" is a reading that falls below the minimum capability of the sensor, and it usually indicates that the sensor has stopped functioning, due to internal or external factors, not that the quantity it measures is actually below the sensor's minimum response value.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:54:25 (EI+616)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> crossed from California into <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> airspace. Speed: Mach 22.5; altitude: 227,400 feet (69.3 km; 43.07 mi).</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Witnesses observed a bright flash at this point and 18 similar events in the next four minutes.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:55:00 (EI+651)</i>: Nearly 11 minutes after <i>Columbia</i> re-entered the atmosphere, wing leading-edge temperatures normally reached nearly 3,000 °F (1,650 °C).</li>
<li><i>8:55:32 (EI+683)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> crossed from Nevada into <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>. Speed: Mach 21.8; altitude: 223,400 feet (68.1 km; 42.31 mi).</li>
<li><i>8:55:52 (EI+703)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> crossed from Utah into <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>.</li>
<li><i>8:56:30 (EI+741)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> began a roll reversal, turning from right to left over Arizona.</li>
<li><i>8:56:45 (EI+756)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> crossed from Arizona to <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>. Speed: Mach 20.9; altitude: 219,000 feet (67 km; 41.5 mi).</li>
<li><i>8:57:24 (EI+795)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> passed just north of <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque</a>.</li>
<li><i>8:58:00 (EI+831)</i>: At this point, wing leading-edge temperatures typically decreased to 2,880 °F (1,580 °C).</li>
<li><i>8:58:20 (EI+851)</i>: <i>Columbia</i> crossed from New Mexico into Texas. Speed: Mach 19.5; altitude: 209,800 feet (63.9 km; 39.73 mi).</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>At about this time, the Orbiter shed a Thermal Protection System tile, the most westerly piece of debris that has been recovered. Searchers found the tile in a field in <a href="/wiki/Littlefield,_Texas" title="Littlefield, Texas">Littlefield, Texas</a>, just northwest of <a href="/wiki/Lubbock,_Texas" title="Lubbock, Texas">Lubbock</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>8:59:15 (EI+906)</i>: MMACS told the Flight Director that pressure readings had been lost on both left main landing-gear tires. The Flight Director then instructed the Capsule Communicator (CAPCOM) to let the crew know that Mission Control saw the messages and was evaluating the indications, and added that the Flight Control Team did not understand the crew's last transmission.</li>
<li><i>8:59:32 (EI+923)</i>: A broken response from the mission commander was recorded: "Roger, uh, bu – [cut off in mid-word] ..." It was the last communication from the crew and the last <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> signal received in Mission Control.</li>
<li><i>8:59:37 (EI+928)</i>: Hydraulic pressure, which is required to move the flight control surfaces, was lost at about 8:59:37. At that time, the Master Alarm would have sounded for the loss of hydraulics, and the shuttle would have begun to lose control, starting to roll and yaw uncontrollably, and the crew would have become aware of the serious problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>9:00:18 (EI+969)</i>: Videos and eyewitness reports by observers on the ground in and near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dallas,_Texas" title="Dallas, Texas">Dallas</a> indicated that the Orbiter had disintegrated overhead, continued to break up into smaller pieces, and left multiple ion trails, as it continued eastward. In Mission Control, while the loss of signal was a cause for concern, there was no sign of any serious problem. Before the orbiter broke up at 9:00:18, the <i>Columbia</i> cabin pressure was nominal and the crew was capable of conscious actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008-24">[24]</a></sup> Although the crew module remained mostly intact through the breakup, it was damaged enough that it lost pressure at a rate fast enough to incapacitate the crew within seconds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and was completely depressurized no later than 9:00:53.</li>
<li><i>9:00:57 (EI+1008)</i>: The crew module, intact to this point, was seen breaking into small subcomponents. It disappeared from view at 9:01:10. The crew, if not already dead, were killed no later than this point.</li>
<li><i>9:05</i>: Residents of north central Texas, particularly near <a href="/wiki/Tyler,_Texas" title="Tyler, Texas">Tyler</a>, reported a loud boom, a small concussion wave, smoke trails and debris in the clear skies above the counties east of Dallas.</li>
<li><i>9:12:39 (EI+1710)</i>: After hearing of reports of the shuttle being seen to break apart, Entry Flight Director LeRoy Cain declared a contingency (events leading to loss of the vehicle) and alerted search-and-rescue teams in the debris area. He called on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_Controller" title="Ground Controller">Ground Controller</a> to "lock the doors", meaning no one would be permitted to enter or leave until everything needed for investigation of the accident had been secured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Two minutes later, Mission Control put contingency procedures into effect.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_survivability_aspects">Crew survivability aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Crew survivability aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2008, NASA released a detailed report on survivability aspects of the <i>Columbia</i> reentry. The crew would have had less than a minute between the beginning of orbiter disintegration and depressurization. The structural failure of the left wing set off alarms in the cabin, although they had no way of knowing that the wing had broken apart as the rear of the orbiter could not be seen from the cabin. All evidence indicated that the crew frantically tried to regain control of <i>Columbia</i> as it began to spin out of control, but the loss of the left wing caused the orbiter to yaw to the right, exposing its underside to extreme aerodynamic forces and causing total structural disintegration. The crew cabin separated from the rest of the orbiter and rapidly depressurized, which would have killed or incapacitated the astronauts within seconds. Afterwards, the cabin spun around at high RPM, which caused the seat restraints on their upper bodies to fail. They were thus whipped around violently and pummeled by flying and falling objects from the disintegrating cabin, along with their heads and necks being slammed against the helmets, which were not designed to provide any head protection. Even if the cabin had remained structurally intact and reached a lower altitude where air could refill it, the high altitude depressurization would have been fatal to the astronauts unless they received medical attention within 5 minutes, approximately the amount of time it would take between cerebral hypoxia and brain death.</p>
<p>After cabin disintegration, the astronauts' bodies were released into the upper atmosphere and battered by extreme aerodynamic forces (close to 14,000 MPH) and temperatures. The remains of the crew then fell some 200,000 feet to earth, where they were also subjected to burning from aerodynamic heating. The official NASA report omitted some of the more graphic details on the recovery of the remains, however witnesses reported various gruesome finds such as a human heart and parts of femur bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>All evidence indicated that crew error was in no way responsible for the disintegration of the orbiter, and they had acted correctly and according to procedure at the first indication of trouble. Although some of the crew were not wearing gloves or helmets during reentry and some were not properly restrained in their seats, doing these things would have added nothing to their survival chances other than perhaps keeping them alive and conscious another 30 or so seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_response">Presidential response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Presidential response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="198.73433106576" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="George_W_Bush_Columbia_FINAL.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="67131" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (67 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/George_W_Bush_Columbia_FINAL.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/>
<track data-dir="ltr" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="TimedText:George_W_Bush_Columbia_FINAL.ogg.en.srt" kind="subtitles" label="English (en) subtitles" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:George_W_Bush_Columbia_FINAL.ogg.en.srt&amp;action=raw&amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" srclang="en" type="text/x-srt"/></audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_W_Bush_Columbia_FINAL.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President George W. Bush's address on the <i>Columbia</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s destruction, February 1, 2003</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 14:04 EST (19:04 UTC), <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">President George W. Bush</a> said, "This day has brought terrible news and great sadness to our country ... The <i>Columbia</i> is lost; there are no survivors". Despite the disaster, Bush said, "The cause in which they died will continue....Our journey into space will go on".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Bush later declared East Texas a federal disaster area, allowing federal agencies to help with the recovery effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery_of_debris">Recovery of debris</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Recovery of debris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1955" data-file-width="3000" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg/220px-Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg/330px-Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg/440px-Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grid_with_Columbia%27s_Debris_-_GPN-2003-00081.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A grid on the floor is used to organize recovered debris</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg"><img alt="refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2160" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg/220px-Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg/330px-Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg/440px-Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbia%27s_Main_Engine_Powerheads_-_GPN-2003-00076.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Recovered power-head of one of Columbia's main engines</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>More than 2,000 debris fields were found in sparsely populated areas from <a href="/wiki/Nacogdoches,_Texas" title="Nacogdoches, Texas">Nacogdoches</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Texas" title="East Texas">East Texas</a>, where a large amount of debris fell, to western <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and the southwestern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counties_of_Arkansas" title="Counties of Arkansas">counties of Arkansas</a>. A large amount of debris was recovered between <a href="/wiki/Tyler,_Texas" title="Tyler, Texas">Tyler, Texas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palestine,_Texas" title="Palestine, Texas">Palestine, Texas</a>. One debris field has been mapped along a path stretching from south of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Worth" title="Fort Worth">Fort Worth</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hemphill,_Texas" title="Hemphill, Texas">Hemphill, Texas</a>, as well as into parts of Louisiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2003_p._41_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2003_p._41-31">[31]</a></sup> Various notable places that had debris included <a href="/wiki/Stephen_F._Austin_State_University" title="Stephen F. Austin State University">Stephen F. Austin State University</a> in Nacogdoches and several casinos in <a href="/wiki/Shreveport,_Louisiana" title="Shreveport, Louisiana">Shreveport, Louisiana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2003_p._41_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2003_p._41-31">[31]</a></sup> Along with pieces of the shuttle and bits of equipment, searchers also found human body parts, including arms, feet, a torso, and a heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the months after the disaster, the largest-ever organized ground search took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-News_Researcher_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-News_Researcher-33">[33]</a></sup> Thousands of volunteers descended upon Texas to participate in the effort to gather the Shuttle’s remains. According to Mike Ciannilli, Project Manager of the Columbia Research and Preservation Office, "[these people] put their life on hold to help out the nation’s space program,” showing “what space means to people.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space_Safety_Magazine_Columbia_Disaster_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space_Safety_Magazine_Columbia_Disaster-34">[34]</a></sup> NASA issued warnings to the public that any debris could contain hazardous chemicals, that it should be left untouched, its location reported to local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_services" title="Emergency services">emergency services</a> or government authorities, and that anyone in unauthorized possession of debris would be prosecuted. Because of the widespread area, volunteer <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator" title="Amateur radio operator">amateur radio operators</a> accompanied the search teams to provide communications support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>A group of small (1 mm) adult <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">Caenorhabditis elegans</a></i> worms, living in <a href="/wiki/Petri_dish" title="Petri dish">petri dishes</a> enclosed in aluminum canisters, survived reentry and impact with the ground and were recovered weeks after the disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The culture was found to be alive on April 28, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The worms were part of a biological research in canisters experiment designed to study the effect of weightlessness on <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>; the experiment was conducted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassie_Conley" title="Cassie Conley">Cassie Conley</a>, NASA's <a href="/wiki/Planetary_protection" title="Planetary protection">planetary protection</a> officer.</p>
<p>Debris Search Pilot Jules F. Mier Jr. and Debris Search Aviation Specialist Charles Krenek died in a helicopter crash that injured three others during the search.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Texas residents recovered some of the debris, ignoring the warnings, and attempted to sell it on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online_auction_business_model" title="Online auction business model">online auction site</a> <a href="/wiki/EBay" title="EBay">eBay</a>, starting at $10,000. The auction was quickly removed, but prices for <i>Columbia</i> merchandise such as programs, photographs and patches, went up dramatically following the disaster, creating a surge of <i>Columbia</i>-related listings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> A three-day amnesty offered for "looted" shuttle debris brought in hundreds of illegally recovered pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> About 40,000 recovered pieces of debris have never been identified. The largest pieces recovered include the front landing gear,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and a window frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="398" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg/220px-STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg/330px-STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-107_Cockpit_Video_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The glow of reentry as seen out the front windows</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 9, 2008, it was reported that data from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disk_drive" title="Disk drive">disk drive</a> on board <i>Columbia</i> had survived the shuttle accident, and while part of the 340 MB drive was damaged, 99% of the data was recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The drive was used to store data from an experiment on the properties of <a href="/wiki/Shear_thinning" title="Shear thinning">shear thinning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 29, 2011, Nacogdoches authorities told NASA that a 4-foot (1.2 m) diameter piece of debris had been found in a lake. NASA identified the piece as a power reactant storage and distribution (PRSD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (November 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>All recovered non-human <i>Columbia</i> debris is stored in unused office space at the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">Vehicle Assembly Building</a>, except for parts of the crew compartment, which are kept separate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_cabin_video">Crew cabin video</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Crew cabin video">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_1" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_2" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/STS-107%2C_final_moments_in_cabin_%28Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster%29.webm/320px--STS-107%2C_final_moments_in_cabin_%28Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster%29.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="470.333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="STS-107,_final_moments_in_cabin_(Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster).webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/STS-107%2C_final_moments_in_cabin_%28Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster%29.webm" type="video/webm;
