codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="193384" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:STS-107, final moments in cabin (Space Shuttle Columbia disaster).webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/STS-107%2C_final_moments_in_cabin_%28Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster%29.webm/220px--STS-107%2C_final_moments_in_cabin_%28Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster%29.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/STS-107%2C_final_moments_in_cabin_%28Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster%29.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-107,_final_moments_in_cabin_(Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster).webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Video taken by the crew of part of re-entry</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Among the recovered items was a videotape recording made by the astronauts during the start of re-entry. The 13-minute recording shows the flight crew astronauts conducting routine re-entry procedures and joking with each other. None gives any indication of a problem. In the video, the flight-deck crew puts on their gloves and passes the video camera around to record <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> and flames visible outside the windows of the orbiter (a normal occurrence). The recording, which on normal flights would have continued through landing, ends about four minutes before the shuttle began to disintegrate and 11 minutes before Mission Control lost the signal from the orbiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigation">Investigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Investigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_investigation">Initial investigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Initial investigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Impact-test.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1648" data-file-width="2464" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Impact-test.jpg/220px-Impact-test.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Impact-test.jpg/330px-Impact-test.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Impact-test.jpg/440px-Impact-test.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Impact-test.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mock-up of a <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> leading edge made with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reinforced_carbon-carbon" title="Reinforced carbon-carbon">RCC</a>-panel taken from <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery"><i>Discovery</i></a>. Simulation of known and possible conditions of the foam impact on <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a>'s final launch showed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brittle_fracture" title="Brittle fracture">brittle fracture</a> of RCC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>NASA Space Shuttle Program Manager <a href="/wiki/Ron_Dittemore" title="Ron Dittemore">Ron Dittemore</a> reported that "The first indication was loss of temperature sensors and hydraulic systems on the left wing. They were followed seconds and minutes later by several other problems, including loss of tire pressure indications on the left main gear and then indications of excessive structural heating".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Analysis of 31 seconds of telemetry data which had initially been filtered out because of data corruption within it showed the shuttle fighting to maintain its orientation, eventually using maximum thrust from its Reaction Control System jets.</p>
<p>The investigation focused on the foam strike from the very beginning. Incidents of debris strikes from ice and foam causing damage during take-off were already well known, and had damaged orbiters, most noticeably during <a href="/wiki/STS-45" title="STS-45">STS-45</a>, <a href="/wiki/STS-27" title="STS-27">STS-27</a>, and <a href="/wiki/STS-87" title="STS-87">STS-87</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> After the loss of <i>Columbia</i>, NASA concluded that mistakes during installation were the likely cause of foam loss, and retrained employees at <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a> in Louisiana to apply foam without defects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hale20120418_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-hale20120418-14">[14]</a></sup> Tile damage had also been traced to ablating insulating material from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> fuel tank in the past. The composition of the foam insulation had been changed in 1997 to exclude the use of <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">freon</a>, a chemical that is suspected to cause <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a>; while NASA was exempted from legislation phasing out CFCs, the agency chose to change the foam nonetheless. STS-107 used an older "lightweight tank" (a design that was succeeded by the "superlightweight tank", both being upgrades from the original space shuttle external tank) where the foam was sprayed on to the larger cylindrical surfaces using the newer freon-free foam. However, the bipod ramps were manufactured from BX-250 foam which was excluded from the EPA regulations and did use the original freon formula. The composition change did not contribute to the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In any case, the original formulation had shown frequent foam losses, as discussed earlier in this article.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board">Columbia Accident Investigation Board</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Columbia Accident Investigation Board">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board" title="Columbia Accident Investigation Board">Columbia Accident Investigation Board</a></div>
<p>Following protocols established after the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">loss</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Challenger</a></i>, an independent investigating board was created immediately after the accident. The Columbia Accident Investigation Board, or CAIB, was chaired by retired US Navy Admiral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harold_W._Gehman,_Jr." title="Harold W. Gehman, Jr.">Harold W. Gehman, Jr.</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and consisted of expert military and civilian analysts who investigated the accident in detail.</p>
<p><i>Columbia</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_data_recorder" title="Flight data recorder">flight data recorder</a> was found near <a href="/wiki/Hemphill,_Texas" title="Hemphill, Texas">Hemphill, Texas</a>, on March 19, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Unlike commercial jet aircraft, the space shuttles did not have flight data recorders intended for after-crash analysis. Instead, the vehicle data were transmitted in real time to the ground via <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a>. Since <i>Columbia</i> was the first shuttle, it had a special flight data OEX (Orbiter EXperiments) recorder, designed to help engineers better understand vehicle performance during the first test flights. After the initial Shuttle test-flights were completed, the recorder was never removed from <i>Columbia</i>, and it was still functioning on the crashed flight. It recorded many hundreds of parameters, and contained very extensive logs of structural and other data, which allowed the CAIB to reconstruct many of the events during the process leading to breakup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Investigators could often use the loss of signals from sensors on the wing to track how the damage progressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-learn_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-learn-55">[55]</a></sup> This was correlated with forensic debris analysis conducted at <a href="/wiki/Lehigh_University" title="Lehigh University">Lehigh University</a> and other tests to obtain a final conclusion about the probable course of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning on May 30, 2003, foam impact tests were performed by <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">Southwest Research Institute</a>. They used a compressed air gun to fire a foam block of similar size and mass to that which struck <i>Columbia</i>, at the same estimated speed. To represent the leading edge of <i>Columbia</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s left wing, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reinforced_carbon-carbon" title="Reinforced carbon-carbon">RCC</a> panels from NASA stock, along with the actual leading-edge panels from <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Enterprise" title="Space Shuttle Enterprise">Enterprise</a></i>, which were fiberglass, were mounted to a simulating structural metal frame. At the beginning of testing, the likely impact site was estimated to be between RCC panel 6 and 9, inclusive. Over many days, dozens of the foam blocks were shot at the wing leading edge model at various angles. These produced only cracks or surface damage to the RCC panels.</p>
<p>During June, further analysis of information from Columbia's flight data recorder narrowed the probable impact site to one single panel: RCC wing panel 8. On July 7, in a final round of testing, a block fired at the side of an RCC panel 8 created a hole 16 by 16.7 inches (41 by 42 cm) in that protective RCC panel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The tests demonstrated that a foam impact of the type <i>Columbia</i> sustained could seriously breach the thermal protection system on the wing leading edge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conclusions">Conclusions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conclusions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On August 26, 2003, the CAIB issued its report on the accident. The report confirmed the immediate cause of the accident was a breach in the leading edge of the left wing, caused by insulating foam shed during launch. The report also delved deeply into the underlying organizational and cultural issues that led to the accident. The report was highly critical of NASA's decision-making and risk-assessment processes. It concluded the organizational structure and processes were sufficiently flawed and that a compromise of safety was expected no matter who was in the key decision-making positions. An example was the position of Shuttle Program Manager, where one individual was responsible for achieving safe, timely launches and acceptable costs, which are often conflicting goals. The CAIB report found that NASA had accepted deviations from design criteria as normal when they happened on several flights and did not lead to mission-compromising consequences. One of those was the conflict between a <a href="/wiki/Design_specification" title="Design specification">design specification</a> stating that the thermal protection system was not designed to withstand significant impacts and the common occurrence of impact damage to it during flight. The board made recommendations for significant changes in processes and organizational culture.</p>
<p>On December 30, 2008, NASA released a further report, entitled Columbia Crew Survival Investigation Report, produced by a second commission, the Spacecraft Crew Survival Integrated Investigation Team (SCSIIT). NASA had commissioned this group, "to perform a comprehensive analysis of the accident, focusing on factors and events affecting crew survival, and to develop recommendations for improving crew survival for all future human space flight vehicles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Layout_1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Layout_1-59">[59]</a></sup> The report concluded that: "The Columbia depressurization event occurred so rapidly that the crew members were incapacitated within seconds, before they could configure the suit for full protection from loss of cabin pressure. Although circulatory systems functioned for a brief time, the effects of the depressurization were severe enough that the crew could not have regained consciousness. This event was lethal to the crew."</p>
<p>The report also concluded:</p>
<ul>
<li>The crew did not have time to prepare themselves. Some crew members were not wearing their safety gloves, and one crew member was not wearing a helmet. New policies gave the crew more time to prepare for descent.</li>
<li>The crew's safety harnesses malfunctioned during the violent descent. The harnesses on the three remaining shuttles were upgraded after the accident.</li>
</ul>
<p>The key recommendations of the report included that future spacecraft crew survival systems should not rely on manual activation to protect the crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_emergency_procedures">Possible emergency procedures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Possible emergency procedures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One question of special importance was whether NASA could have saved the astronauts had they known of the danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This would have to involve either rescue or repair – docking at the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> for use as a haven while awaiting rescue (or to use the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> to systematically ferry the crew to safety) would have been impossible due to the different <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">orbital inclination</a> of the vehicles.</p>
<p>The CAIB determined that a rescue mission, though risky, might have been possible provided NASA management had taken action soon enough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIChap6Page173_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIChap6Page173-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12-63">[63]</a></sup> Normally, a rescue mission is not possible, due to the time required to prepare a shuttle for launch, and the limited consumables (power, water, air) of an orbiting shuttle. However, <i>Atlantis</i> was well along in processing for a planned March 1 launch on <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a>, and <i>Columbia</i> carried an unusually large quantity of consumables due to an <a href="/wiki/Extended_Duration_Orbiter" title="Extended Duration Orbiter">Extended Duration Orbiter</a> package. The CAIB determined that this would have allowed <i>Columbia</i> to stay in orbit until flight day 30 (February 15). NASA investigators determined that <i>Atlantis</i> processing could have been expedited with no skipped safety checks for a February 10 launch. Hence, if nothing went wrong, there was a five-day overlap for a possible rescue. As mission control could deorbit an empty shuttle, but could not control the orbiter's reentry and landing, it would likely have sent <i>Columbia</i> into the Pacific Ocean;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12-63">[63]</a></sup> NASA later developed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Remote_Control_Orbiter" title="Remote Control Orbiter">Remote Control Orbiter</a> system to permit mission control to land a shuttle.</p>
<p>NASA investigators determined that on-orbit repair by the shuttle astronauts was possible but overall considered "high risk", primarily due to the uncertain resiliency of the repair using available materials and the anticipated high risk of doing additional damage to the Orbiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIChap6Page173_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIChap6Page173-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>Columbia</i> did not carry the <a href="/wiki/Canadarm" title="Canadarm">Canadarm</a>, or Remote Manipulator System, which would normally be used for camera inspection or transporting a spacewalking astronaut to the wing. Therefore, an unusual emergency <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">extra-vehicular activity</a> (EVA) would have been required. While there was no astronaut EVA training for maneuvering to the wing, astronauts are always prepared for a similarly difficult emergency EVA to close the external tank umbilical doors located on the orbiter underside, which is necessary for reentry. Similar methods could have reached the shuttle left wing for inspection or repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the repair, the CAIB determined that the astronauts would have to use tools and small pieces of titanium, or other metal, scavenged from the crew cabin. These metals would help protect the wing structure and would be held in place during re-entry by a water-filled bag that had turned into ice in the cold of space. The ice and metal would help restore wing leading edge geometry, preventing a turbulent airflow over the wing and therefore keeping heating and burn-through levels low enough for the crew to survive re-entry and bail out before landing. The CAIB could not determine whether a patched-up left wing would have survived even a modified re-entry, and concluded that the rescue option would have had a considerably higher chance of bringing <i>Columbia</i>'s crew back alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIChap6Page173_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIChap6Page173-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-CAIBVolIIAppendixD12-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On February 4, 2003, President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Laura_Bush" title="Laura Bush">Laura</a> led a memorial service for the astronauts' families at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center</a>. Two days later, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">Dick Cheney</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Cheney" title="Lynne Cheney">Lynne</a> led a similar service at <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">Washington National Cathedral</a>. <a href="/wiki/Patti_LaBelle" title="Patti LaBelle">Patti LaBelle</a> sang "Way Up There" as part of the service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3032" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg/220px-Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg/330px-Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg/440px-Columbia_makeshift_memorial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County,_Texas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2257" data-file-width="5522" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County%2C_Texas.jpg/220px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County%2C_Texas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County%2C_Texas.jpg/330px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County%2C_Texas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County%2C_Texas.jpg/440px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_memorial_-_Shelby_County%2C_Texas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

Space Shuttle Columbia memorial – Sabine County, Texas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On February 2, 2003, and throughout March, April, and May 2003, large memorial Catholic Brazilian masses and Roman Catholic memorial concerts were held in <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sao_Paulo" title="Sao Paulo">Sao Paulo</a>, and other cities in Brazil where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_people" title="Brazilian people">Brazilian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priest</a> <a href="/wiki/Marcelo_Rossi" title="Marcelo Rossi">Marcelo Rossi</a> and his concert partner Belo sang a Christian hymn "Noites Traicoeiras" (Treacherous Nights) as tribute to the seven Columbia astronauts, as well as the other seven crew members who lost their lives in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Space Shuttle Challenger disaster</a> in 1986. The concerts were televised to millions throughout Brazil and the world.</p>
<p>On March 26, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>' Science Committee approved funds for the construction of a memorial at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a> for the STS-107 crew. A similar memorial was built at the cemetery for the last crew of <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger"><i>Challenger</i></a>. On October 28, 2003, the names of the astronauts were added to the <a href="/wiki/Space_Mirror_Memorial" title="Space Mirror Memorial">Space Mirror Memorial</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Visitor_Complex" title="Kennedy Space Center Visitor Complex">Kennedy Space Center Visitor Complex</a>.</p>
<p>On April 1, 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Opening_Day" title="Opening Day">Opening Day</a> of <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> season, the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Astros" title="Houston Astros">Houston Astros</a> (named in honor of the U.S. space program) honored the <i>Columbia</i> crew by having seven simultaneous <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_first_pitch" title="Ceremonial first pitch">first pitches</a> thrown by family and friends of the crew. For the <a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">National Anthem</a>, 107 NASA personnel, including flight controllers and others involved in <i>Columbia</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s final mission, carried a U.S. flag onto the field. In addition, the Astros wore the mission patch on their sleeves and replaced all dugout advertising with the mission patch logo for the entire season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 1, 2004, the first anniversary of the <i>Columbia</i> disaster, <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXXVIII" title="Super Bowl XXXVIII">Super Bowl XXXVIII</a> held in Houston's <a href="/wiki/NRG_Stadium" title="NRG Stadium">Reliant Stadium</a> began with a pregame tribute to the crew of the <i>Columbia</i> by singer <a href="/wiki/Josh_Groban" title="Josh Groban">Josh Groban</a> performing "<a href="/wiki/You_Raise_Me_Up" title="You Raise Me Up">You Raise Me Up</a>", with the crew of <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a>, the first post-<i>Columbia</i> Space Shuttle mission, in attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, Bush conferred posthumous <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Space_Medal_of_Honor" title="Congressional Space Medal of Honor">Congressional Space Medals of Honor</a> to all 14 crew members lost in the <i>Challenger</i> and <i>Columbia</i> accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>NASA named several places in honor of <i>Columbia</i> and the crew. Seven <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> discovered in July 2001 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Palomar_observatory" title="Mount Palomar observatory">Mount Palomar observatory</a> were officially given the names of the seven astronauts: <a href="/wiki/51823_Rickhusband" title="51823 Rickhusband">51823 Rickhusband</a>, <a href="/wiki/51824_Mikeanderson" title="51824 Mikeanderson">51824 Mikeanderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/51825_Davidbrown" title="51825 Davidbrown">51825 Davidbrown</a>, <a href="/wiki/51826_Kalpanachawla" title="51826 Kalpanachawla">51826 Kalpanachawla</a>, <a href="/wiki/51827_Laurelclark" title="51827 Laurelclark">51827 Laurelclark</a>, <a href="/wiki/51828_Ilanramon" title="51828 Ilanramon">51828 Ilanramon</a>, <a href="/wiki/51829_Williemccool" title="51829 Williemccool">51829 Williemccool</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, the landing site of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover_Mission" title="Mars Exploration Rover Mission">rover</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MER-A" title="MER-A">Spirit</a></i> was named <i>Columbia</i> Memorial Station, and included a memorial plaque to the <i>Columbia</i> crew mounted on the back of the high gain antenna. A complex of seven hills east of the <i>Spirit</i> landing site was dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Hills_(Mars)" title="Columbia Hills (Mars)">Columbia Hills</a>; each of the seven hills was individually named for a member of the crew, and <a href="/wiki/Husband_Hill" title="Husband Hill">Husband Hill</a> in particular was ascended and explored by the rover. In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">IAU</a> approved naming of a cluster of seven small craters in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_(crater)" title="Apollo (crater)">Apollo basin</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a> after the astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Back on Earth, NASA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Scientific_Balloon_Facility" title="National Scientific Balloon Facility">National Scientific Balloon Facility</a> was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Scientific_Balloon_Facility" title="Columbia Scientific Balloon Facility">Columbia Scientific Balloon Facility</a>.</p>
<p>Other tributes included the decision by <a href="/wiki/Amarillo,_Texas" title="Amarillo, Texas">Amarillo, Texas</a>, to rename its airport <a href="/wiki/Rick_Husband_Amarillo_International_Airport" title="Rick Husband Amarillo International Airport">Rick Husband Amarillo International Airport</a> after the Amarillo native. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Route_904_(Washington)" title="State Route 904 (Washington)">Washington State Route 904</a> was renamed Lt. Michael P. Anderson Memorial Highway, as it runs through <a href="/wiki/Cheney,_Washington" title="Cheney, Washington">Cheney, Washington</a>, the town where he graduated from high school. A newly constructed elementary school located on Fairchild Air Force Base near <a href="/wiki/Spokane,_Washington" title="Spokane, Washington">Spokane, Washington</a>, was named Michael Anderson Elementary School. Anderson had attended fifth grade at Blair Elementary, the base's previous elementary school, while his father was stationed there. A mountain peak near <a href="/wiki/Kit_Carson_Peak" title="Kit Carson Peak">Kit Carson Peak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Challenger_Point" title="Challenger Point">Challenger Point</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sangre_de_Cristo_Range" title="Sangre de Cristo Range">Sangre de Cristo Range</a> was renamed <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Point" title="Columbia Point">Columbia Point</a>, and a dedication plaque was placed on the point in August, 2003. Seven dormitories were named in honor of <i>Columbia</i> crew members at the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Institute_of_Technology" title="Florida Institute of Technology">Florida Institute of Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Creighton_University" title="Creighton University">Creighton University</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_University_of_Texas_at_Arlington" title="The University of Texas at Arlington">The University of Texas at Arlington</a>, and the Columbia Elementary School in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brevard_County_School_District" title="Brevard County School District">Brevard County School District</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Huntsville_City_Schools" title="Huntsville City Schools">Huntsville City Schools</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>, a city strongly associated with NASA, named their most recent high school Columbia High School as a memorial to the crew. A Department of Defense school in Guam was renamed Commander William C. McCool Elementary School.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The City of <a href="/wiki/Palmdale,_California" title="Palmdale, California">Palmdale, California</a>, the birthplace of the entire shuttle fleet, changed the name of the thoroughfare Avenue M to Columbia Way. In Avondale, Arizona, the Avondale Elementary School where Michael Anderson's sister worked had sent a T-shirt with him into space. It was supposed to have an assembly when he returned from space. The school was later renamed Michael Anderson Elementary.</p>
<p><span id="downey"></span> The first dedicated meteorological satellite launched by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> (ISRO) on 2 September 2002, named Metsat-1, was later renamed <a href="/wiki/Kalpana-1" title="Kalpana-1">Kalpana-1</a> by Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Atal_Bihari_Vajpayee" title="Atal Bihari Vajpayee">Atal Bihari Vajpayee</a> in memory of India-born Kalpana Chawla.</p>
<p>In October 2004, both houses of Congress passed a resolution authored by US Representative <a href="/wiki/Lucille_Roybal-Allard" title="Lucille Roybal-Allard">Lucille Roybal-Allard</a> and co-sponsored by the entire contingent of California representatives to Congress changing the name of <a href="/wiki/Downey,_California" title="Downey, California">Downey, California</a>'s Space Science Learning Center to the Columbia Memorial Space Science Learning Center. The facility is located at the former manufacturing site of the space shuttles, including <i>Columbia</i> and <i>Challenger</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Air_Force" title="US Air Force">US Air Force</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Squadron_Officer_School" title="Squadron Officer School">Squadron Officer School</a> at <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Air_Force_Base" title="Maxwell Air Force Base">Maxwell Air Force Base</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, renamed their auditorium in Husband's honor. He was a graduate of the program. The US Test Pilot School at <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a> in California named its pilot lounge for Husband.</p>
<p>NASA named a <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">supercomputer</a> "<a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercomputer)" title="Columbia (supercomputer)">Columbia</a>" in the crew's honor in 2004. It was located at the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Advanced_Supercomputing_Division" title="NASA Advanced Supercomputing Division">NASA Advanced Supercomputing Division</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a> on <a href="/wiki/Moffett_Federal_Airfield" title="Moffett Federal Airfield">Moffett Federal Airfield</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View, California</a>. The first part of the system, built in 2003, known as "<a href="/wiki/Kalpana_(supercomputer)" title="Kalpana (supercomputer)">Kalpana</a>" was dedicated to Chawla, who worked at Ames prior to joining the Space Shuttle program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>A US Navy compound at a major coalition military base in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> is named Camp McCool. In addition, the athletic field at McCool's alma mater, <a href="/wiki/Coronado_High_School_(Lubbock,_Texas)" title="Coronado High School (Lubbock, Texas)">Coronado High School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lubbock,_Texas" title="Lubbock, Texas">Lubbock, Texas</a>, was renamed the Willie McCool Track and Field.</p>
<p>A proposed reservoir in <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_County,_Texas" title="Cherokee County, Texas">Cherokee County</a> in Eastern Texas is to be named Lake Columbia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ilan Ramon High School was established in 2006 in <a href="/wiki/Hod_HaSharon" title="Hod HaSharon">Hod HaSharon, Israel</a>, in tribute to the first Israeli astronaut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The school's symbol shows the planet Earth with an aircraft orbiting around it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Naval_Medical_Center" title="National Naval Medical Center">National Naval Medical Center</a> dedicated Laurel Clark Memorial Auditorium on July 11, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Phi_Beta" title="Gamma Phi Beta">Gamma Phi Beta</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sorority" title="Sorority">sorority</a>, of which Clark was a member, created the Laurel Clark Foundation in her honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>PS 58 in Staten Island, New York, was named Space Shuttle Columbia School in honor of the failed mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Challenger_Columbia_Stadium" title="Challenger Columbia Stadium">Challenger Columbia Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/League_City,_Texas" title="League City, Texas">League City, Texas</a> is named in honor of the victims of both the <i>Columbia</i> disaster as well as the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"><i>Challenger</i> disaster</a> in 1986.</p>
<p>Columbia Colles, a range of hills on <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> discovered by the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> spacecraft in July 2015, was named in honor of the victims of the disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>A starship on <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Enterprise" title="Star Trek: Enterprise">Star Trek: Enterprise</a></i> was named U.S.S. <i>Columbia</i> in honor of the <i>Columbia</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_space_programs">Effect on space programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Effect on space programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Following the loss of <i>Columbia</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle_program" title="Space shuttle program">space shuttle program</a> was suspended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-learn_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-learn-55">[55]</a></sup> The further construction of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) was also delayed, as the space shuttles were the only available delivery vehicle for station modules. The station was supplied using Russian unmanned <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progress_spacecraft" title="Progress spacecraft">Progress</a></i> ships, and crews were exchanged using Russian-manned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soyuz_spacecraft" title="Soyuz spacecraft"><i>Soyuz</i> spacecraft</a>, and forced to operate on a skeleton crew of two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Less than a year after the accident, President Bush announced the <a href="/wiki/Vision_for_Space_Exploration" title="Vision for Space Exploration">Vision for Space Exploration</a>, calling for the space shuttle fleet to complete the ISS, with retirement by the year 2010 following the completion of the ISS, to be replaced by a newly developed <a href="/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle" title="Crew Exploration Vehicle">Crew Exploration Vehicle</a> for travel to the Moon and Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> NASA planned to return the space shuttle to service around September 2004; that date was pushed back to July 2005.</p>
<p>On July 26, 2005, at 10:39 am EST, Space Shuttle <i>Discovery</i> cleared the tower on the "Return to Flight" mission <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a>, marking the shuttle's return to space. Overall the STS-114 flight was highly successful, but a similar piece of foam from a different portion of the tank was shed, although the debris did not strike the Orbiter. Due to this, NASA once again grounded the shuttles until the remaining problem was understood and a solution implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-learn_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-learn-55">[55]</a></sup> After delaying their re-entry by two days due to adverse weather conditions, Commander <a href="/wiki/Eileen_Collins" title="Eileen Collins">Eileen Collins</a> and Pilot <a href="/wiki/James_M._Kelly_(astronaut)" title="James M. Kelly (astronaut)">James M. Kelly</a> returned <i>Discovery</i> safely to Earth on August 9, 2005. Later that same month, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">external tank</a> construction site at Michoud was damaged by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> At the time, there was concern that this would set back further shuttle flights by at least two months and possibly more.</p>
<p>The actual cause of the foam loss on both <i>Columbia</i> and <i>Discovery</i> was not determined until December 2005, when <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">x-ray photographs</a> of another tank showed that <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> and contraction during filling, not human error, caused cracks that led to foam loss. NASA's Hale formally apologized to the Michoud workers who had been blamed for the loss of <i>Columbia</i> for almost three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hale20120418_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-hale20120418-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second "Return to Flight" mission, <a href="/wiki/STS-121" title="STS-121">STS-121</a>, launched on July 4, 2006, at 2:37:55 pm (EDT), after two previous launch attempts were scrubbed because of lingering thunderstorms and high winds around the launch pad. The launch took place despite objections from its chief engineer and safety head. This mission increased the ISS crew to three. A 5-inch (130 mm) crack in the foam insulation of the external tank gave cause for concern; however, the Mission Management Team gave the go for launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Space Shuttle <i>Discovery</i> touched down successfully on July 17, 2006 at 9:14:43 am (EDT) on Runway 15 at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>.</p>
<p>On August 13, 2006, NASA announced that STS-121 had shed more foam than they had expected. While this did not delay the launch for the next mission – <a href="/wiki/STS-115" title="STS-115">STS-115</a>, originally set to lift off on August 27 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> – the weather and other technical glitches did, with a lightning strike, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ernesto_(2006)" title="Hurricane Ernesto (2006)">Hurricane Ernesto</a> and a faulty fuel tank sensor combining to delay the launch until September 9. On September 19, landing was delayed an extra day to examine <i>Atlantis</i> after objects were found floating near the shuttle in the same orbit. When no damage was detected, <i>Atlantis</i> landed successfully on September 21.</p>
<p>The <i>Columbia</i> Crew Survival Investigation Report released by NASA on December 30, 2008, made further recommendations to improve a crew's survival chances on future space vehicles, such as the (planned) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_spacecraft" title="Orion spacecraft">Orion spacecraft</a>. These included improvements in crew restraints, finding ways to deal more effectively with catastrophic cabin depressurization, more "graceful degradation" of vehicles during a disaster so that crews will have a better chance at survival, and automated parachute systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Layout_1_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Layout_1-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociocultural_aftermath">Sociocultural aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sociocultural aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fears_of_terrorism">Fears of terrorism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fears of terrorism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the shuttle's breakup, there were some initial fears that terrorists might have been involved, but no evidence of that has ever surfaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Security surrounding the launch and landing of the space shuttle had been increased because the crew included the first Israeli astronaut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merritt_Island" title="Merritt Island">Merritt Island</a> launch facility, like all sensitive government areas, had increased security after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purple_streak_image">Purple streak image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Purple streak image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a></i> reported that an amateur astronomer had taken a five-second exposure that appeared to show "a purplish line near the shuttle", resembling lightning, during re-entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The CAIB report concluded that the image was the result of "camera vibrations during a long-exposure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2003_Armageddon_film_hoax">2003 <i>Armageddon</i> film hoax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 2003 Armageddon film hoax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In response to the disaster, <a href="/wiki/FX_(TV_channel)" title="FX (TV channel)">FX</a> canceled its scheduled airing two nights later of the 1998 film <i><a href="/wiki/Armageddon_(1998_film)" title="Armageddon (1998 film)">Armageddon</a></i>, in which the space shuttle <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i> is depicted as being destroyed by asteroid fragments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In a hoax inspired by the destruction of <i>Columbia</i>, some images that were purported to be satellite photographs of the shuttle's "explosion" turned out to be screen captures from the space shuttle destruction scene of <i>Armageddon</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 2003 album <i><a href="/wiki/Bananas_(album)" title="Bananas (album)">Bananas</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a> includes "Contact Lost", an <a href="/wiki/Instrumental" title="Instrumental">instrumental</a> piece written by guitarist <a href="/wiki/Steve_Morse" title="Steve Morse">Steve Morse</a> in remembrance of the loss. Morse is donating his songwriting royalties to the families of the astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuitarSite_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuitarSite-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Catherine Faber and Callie Hills (the <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a> group known as Echo's Children) included a memorial song titled <i>Columbia</i> on their 2004 album <i>From the Hazel Tree.</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2005 album <i><a href="/wiki/Ultimatum_(EP)" title="Ultimatum (EP)">Ultimatum – EP</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/The_Long_Winters" title="The Long Winters">The Long Winters</a> contains the song "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Commander_Thinks_Aloud" title="The Commander Thinks Aloud">The Commander Thinks Aloud</a>", which was songwriter/singer <a href="/wiki/John_Roderick_(musician)" title="John Roderick (musician)">John Roderick</a>'s musing on the crew's perspective of the unexpected catastrophe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Long_Winters_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Long_Winters-94">[94]</a></sup> In addition, the January 30, 2015 episode of <a href="/wiki/Hrishikesh_Hirway" title="Hrishikesh Hirway">Hrishikesh Hirway</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Song_Exploder" title="Song Exploder"><i>Song Exploder</i> podcast</a> presented an interview with John Roderick about the songwriting and recording process for "The Commander Thinks Aloud".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Song_Exploder.2C_Episode_28_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Song_Exploder.2C_Episode_28-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Swiss post-rock band <a href="/wiki/The_Evpatoria_Report" title="The Evpatoria Report">The Evpatoria Report</a> used the records of radio communication between <i>Columbia</i> and CAPCOM as a sample for their instrumental song <i>Taijin Kyofusho</i> on their 2005 album <a href="/wiki/Golevka_(album)" title="Golevka (album)"><i>Golevka</i></a>.</p>
<p>The Hungarian composer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s" title="Peter Eötvös">Peter Eötvös</a> wrote a piece named <i>Seven</i> for solo violin and orchestra in 2006 in memory of the crew of <i>Columbia</i>. <i>Seven</i> was premiered in 2007 by violinist <a href="/wiki/Akiko_Suwanai" title="Akiko Suwanai">Akiko Suwanai</a>, conducted by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Boulez" title="Pierre Boulez">Pierre Boulez</a>, and it was recorded in 2012 with violinist <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Kopatchinskaja" title="Patricia Kopatchinskaja">Patricia Kopatchinskaja</a> and the composer conducting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seven_by_Peter_E.C3.B6tv.C3.B6s_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seven_by_Peter_E.C3.B6tv.C3.B6s-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2008 album <i>Columbia: We Dare to Dream</i> by Anne Cabrera was written as a tribute to space shuttle <i>Columbia</i> <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">STS-107</a>, the crew, support teams, recovery teams, and the crew's families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_page_of_CCR_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_page_of_CCR-97">[97]</a></sup> A copy of the album on <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">compact disc</a> was flown aboard <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery">space shuttle <i>Discovery</i></a> mission <a href="/wiki/STS-131" title="STS-131">STS-131</a> to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> by astronaut <a href="/wiki/Clayton_Anderson" title="Clayton Anderson">Clayton Anderson</a> in April 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twitpic_Columbia_photo_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-twitpic_Columbia_photo-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Scottish Folk-Rock band <a href="/wiki/Runrig" title="Runrig">Runrig</a> included a song entitled "Somewhere" on their album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_(Runrig_album)" title="The Story (Runrig album)">The Story</a></i> (2016); the song was dedicated to Laurel Clark (who had become a fan of the band during her Navy service in Scotland), and includes a piece of her wake up song, followed by some radio chatter, at the end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008_24-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board_2008_24-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Columbia Accident Investigation Board (2008). <a class="external text" href="http://www.nasa.gov/pdf/298870main_SP-2008-565.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Columbia Crew Survival Investigation Report"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpace+Shuttle+Columbia+disaster&amp;rft.au=Columbia+Accident+Investigation+Board&amp;rft.btitle=Columbia+Crew+Survival+Investigation+Report&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nasa.gov%2Fpdf%2F298870main_SP-2008-565.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.nasa.gov/pdf/298870main_SP-2008-565.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Columbia Crew Survival Investigation Report"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. <i>NASA.gov</i>. 2008. pp. 2–90. <q>The 51-L Challenger accident investigation showed that the Challenger CM remained intact and the crew was able to take some immediate actions after vehicle breakup, although the loads experienced were much higher as a result of the aerodynamic loads (estimated at 16 G to 21 G).5 The Challenger crew became incapacitated quickly and could not complete activation of all breathing air systems, leading to the conclusion that an incapacitating cabin depressurization occurred. By comparison, the Columbia crew experienced lower loads (~3.5 G) at the CE. The fact that none of the crew members lowered their visors strongly suggests that the crew was incapacitated after the CE by a rapid depressurization. Although no quantitative conclusion can be made regarding the cabin depressurization rate, it is probable that the cabin depressurization rate was high enough to incapacitate the crew in a matter of seconds. Conclusion L1-5. The depressurization incapacitated the crew members so rapidly that they were not able to lower their helmet visors.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpace+Shuttle+Columbia+disaster&amp;rft.atitle=Columbia+Crew+Survival+Investigation+Report&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=NASA.gov&amp;rft.pages=2-90&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nasa.gov%2Fpdf%2F298870main_SP-2008-565.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
